0756404738|4.0|Others have already said more that can be properly said about how good this book is, but a review would be rather worthless if I didn't give a few good points before rolling out the bad.    The more Kvothe reveals about himself, the more complex he becomes. While the tenants of what drive his character are fairly standard, as laid out in The Name of the Wind, in The Wise Man's Fear, it's more than that. He is no longer driven only by his desire to seek the Chandrian, and his boyish enthusiasm is reducing like sauce in a pot. He is still compulsive, still witty and clever, but his determination has become much more mature. His actions are now weighted not by outside events, but by his own self-made past. And while he still possesses a healthy amount of idiocy to go along with his numerous geniuses, the core of his character remains the same. This is splendid and subtle work. If any still harbor the mistaken illusion of Kvothe's status as a Mary Sue, The Wise Man's Fear should dispel that.    The world expands quite a bit, which is impressive considering how much ground was tread in The Name of the Wind. Since the specifics of sygaldry and sympathy were laid out in detail prior, there is little page space wasted on retreading it, outside of one or two discussions. But the Four Corners is slowly being filled with detail. What were before offered as dialogue asides or quick mentions by Kvothe in the first book, in this one the ground is actually being tread, and we're being shown the differences in culture, the differences in storytelling, the differences in folklore. Though the folklore isn't as weighty a presence as it was in The Name of the Wind, it is still striking and still extremely compelling.    The plot is wonderfully constructed, and though the book shares some of the excesses of the first one, the plot is different enough and deep enough to keep a reader's attention even through parts that may drag along a little too far. This is no simple three-act-and-outstory; it possesses a complexity that is well told enough to resemble real life in a fantastical world. The same is true in this book as it was in its progenitor: while the trappings of fantasy are very present down to the window-dressings, the plot itself ably subverts typical genre clichs while staying true to the roots of the genre.    There is quite a bit more darkness in this go-round as well. The Name of the Wind tended to hint more at the darker edges of the world without ever really exploring them. That is not so in Wise Man's Fear. Rape and banditry and murder are front and center in the main plot, and Kvothe doesn't simply watch it happening, or hear of it through word of mouth. In some instances, Kvothe himself partakes. Make no mistake, this is a book for adults. It never strays too deeply into the heavier elements to turn a reader away, though, and compared with a few other contemporary fantasy authors, Rothfuss' treatment of the violence is surprisingly PG-13.    But for all that, and I could go on quite a lot longer, saying what other people have already said, there are faults. Lots of them. To follow up a small complaint I had with The Name of the Wind: like Kvothe at \\"interesting fact,\\" the map is crap. It is too vague to be of any real use outside of locating Tarbean, The University, and Imre. Local details in any flavor are completely overlooked, giving us a map so broad as to be next to useless in any real sense. The story would function perfectly well without it.    The excesses, while excusable, are much more present in this volume than in the first. While The Name of the Wind was new and unique enough to turn even an old hand at fantasy into a wide-eyed child delighting in finding something new and shiny to look at, The Wise Man's Fear has lost some of the wonder of the setting. Plot asides and tangential stories are slightly more tiring, and some of them get an extremely close focus while other are breezed over as being unimportant. Skipping the shipwreck in favor of spending at least two hundred pages in the Ademre, for instance, is understandable, but the book still drags a bit during his stay with the Adem. The time we spend being told of all the little nuances speaks more of Rothfuss' desire to show a bit more of his (undeniably fascinating) culture than it does of Kvothe's need to get that part of the story told. I would much rather know how his lute case saved his life than be shown a whole lot of vague and melodramatically named sword-stances.    Not to say that it isn't enjoyable, but I feel like it underlies a larger fault of the book as a whole: it is simply too long. 994 hardcover pages isn't a novel, it's a tome. With a few tweaks, it could easily be two much more thoroughly detailed books, but I respect Rothfuss for trimming where he could to get his series in at a healthy three books, instead of dragging us along for four or five or, sticking with his love of folklore, seven entries into Kvothe's story. But it must be said that there is more to this book than there needed to be. While any sacrifice would likely lead to the same complaint (glossing over one area in favor of another, etc), the volume of story we're told is simply staggering, and were it not for Rothfuss' ability to pack in pages worth of detail in a few paragraphs, the book would likely be 1200 pages instead of just 1000.    The third-person framing story still has a jarring difference in tone and diction than the first-person narrative, as well. The framing chapters come off as slightly cartoonish and heavily melodramatic. Kvothe, for instance, says \\"Bast\\" nearly every time he talks to his friend. And not just once or twice in a scatter of dialogue, no: once in each line. This is either something incredibly subtle that Kvothe has to do in order to keep Bast under control, which I am entirely willing to concede as a possibility, or it is a glaring error of proofreading. Either way, the constant repetition is annoying and either needs to be trimmed or explained in the final installment. Bast himself behaves rather more like a puppet, leaping up and down and shouting quite a lot more than is necessary. A little bit of subtlety during the framing scenes would make a dramatic improvement to the book overall.    As a story of a storyteller telling his own story, the book obviously caters to a few excesses. Elements of Rothfuss' worldbuilding certainly fall into this category, as Kvothe goes out of his way to explain things that should be assumed by a member of that world; he even mentions this on one occasion, but seems to ignore his own advice. But for all that, it hardly matters. More worrying is the constant air of mysterious cleverness that Kvothe always maintains, in the first-person and third-person narratives. His encounter on his way back to Severen from Ademre, for instance, is told in a way that makes little sense. Where before Kvothe had always been careful to preface certain surprising actions with a bit of a lead up - look to Kvothe's numerous allusions to his and Ambrose's increasingly dangerous feud in The Name of the Wind for contrast - in this case he just brashly overtreads the subtle details, and the reader is left holding one end of a bloodied sword before he explains what was going on. There certainly are subtle details, but these are details that would work much better in a third-person narrative than a first-person story, especially given the hindsight-granted omniscience that Kvothe wields. The scene worked, and it was surprising, but I think it was surprising for the wrong reasons.     On the same note, Rothfuss seems to go out of his way to make things much more mysterious than I think they need to be. To me, it seems as if the stage crew come out and begin moving the scenery offstage and roll the curtains forward during the middle of a scene; it doesn't really add to mystery, it just nakedly reveals that these elements are Super Mysterious and cannot be revealeduntil act IV. The story could deal with a few more tangible facts and not suffer from it, considering how little we know of what is going on with Kvothe in the \\"present,\\" what is going on in the wider world. We don't even know where the Waystone is, and that is a striking lack of clarity for a story that is usually brimming with detail. Furthermore, important characters who had been implicated in extremely important (not to mention jacket-mentioned) plot points are dealt with offstage with little but a few offhand remarks, which was a little disappointing.    And there are small surface issues as well; a few misspellings and a smattering of small typos, but nothing book-breaking. For all that, The Wise Man's Fear is still an incredibly rich and detailed read, and it ranks among the best fantasy of the past decade for a certainty. Mistakes and all - all of which are arguable, of course - Rothfuss is still a gifted storyteller and his character is even more fascinating this time around. I eagerly await the release of The Kingkiller Chronicles, Day Three, and I anticipate more than a few rereads of The Wise Man's Fear in the future.

B003UESJF6|4.0|I have to admit it; I think I let my expectations for this one get a little out of control.  As out of control as Megamind's ambitions of world domination? Well maybe not quite that bad, but still, despite plans of doing my best to maintain a level head going in, I was pretty swept up in the hype surrounding this big budget computer animated feature.  So did it live up to such stratospheric expectations you ask?  Surprisingly, yes it did and I think a lot of it had to do with the fact that the film does a fantastic job of referencing the little nuances that comic book heroes (or most pertinently, villains) have to endure to keep the hero, well, heroic.<br /><br />Kinda like another tale we're all familiar with, this film opens with a world in eminent danger of being sucked into a black hole and some loving parents placing their infant child into an escape pod.  With his pet/ protector (a fish named Minion), young Megamind (Will Ferrell) finds himself hurdling toward a small blue planet at the edge of the Milky Way only his exodus isn't the only one taking place.<br /><br />Apparently another planet, one with very human-like inhabitants, had very similar ambitions for the survival of their species and sent a pod of their own.<br /><br />In true born loser fashion, this second pod enjoys a smooth trip to Earth and lands on the property of a rich loving couple while Megamind's route traverses endless asteroid belts before finally landing within the gated walls of a prison.  With very different upbringings, a rivalry is born between the two alien refugees only unlike the do-gooding Metro Man (Brad Pitt), Megamind has no real powers save for a massive brain.  Since Metro Man has the \\"city's good guy\\" role pretty well filled, Megamind has little choice but to take up villainy.<br /><br />Despite a pretty good heart, Megamind finds himself repeating tried and true schemes like kidnapping news reporter, Roxanne Ritchi (Tina Fey) only to end up in a custom-painted cellblock in Metro City's prison.<br /><br />Things take an incredible twist when the villain actually gets the upper hand and the reality of the hero/villain symbiotic relationship is finally made clear to Megamind.<br /><br />There's a few central questions behind Megamind, that actually serve as the catalyst for the entire prose, many of which fans of comic books will really take to heart.  Sure on the surface what we're looking at is essentially a spoof at the Superman/ Lex Luthor relationship (with a little Lois Lane thrown in for good measure) only unlike the famous DC tale, DreamWorks slaps a few welcomed twisted to the equation.  Not the least of these is what if the bad guy didn't want to be evil so much as it was forced upon him from the onset.  Another really explores the idea that the relationship between the hero and the villain is one that demands the presence of the other to work.<br /><br />The latter has been explored in films like 1999's Mystery Men but the point is really driven home here in MegaMind.<br /><br />The comedy feels a little bit strained at times but the cast here just plays so well off one another that laughter's inevitable regardless of the viewer's mood going in.  Will Farrell dazzles with his trademark blend of casual dryness and additional props are due to David Cross as Minion and Jonah Hill as Titan.<br /><br />Pacing is quit spot on, and actually succeeds over Pixar's superhero attempt \\"The Incredibles\\"- which did bog down in spots.  Megamind is consistently lively, enthusiastic and visually rich.<br /><br />Hans Zimmer is responsible for the scoring here and does his usual charming work even if the softer tones are often lost to the wailing guitars of Guns n Roses and AC/DC (even the late Michael Jackson isn't overlooked on this explosively charged soundtrack).<br /><br />About the biggest complaint I've located surrounding this film is that the whole genre has been covered by Pixar's Incredibles and Universal's Despicable Me and thisis a valid concern, although a legitimate argument can be made that compared directly against Pixar's 2004 entry, these latter two pieces serve as reminders to the ever-advancing computer generated film industry.  From a visual standpoint, the small details are just outstanding.  Perfection of facial systems, textures and lighting simulations are evident in nearly every frame here.<br /><br />In all I have to confess that DreamWorks' collective grasp on the medium of computer generated feature filmmaking is subtly becoming masterful, if undeniably commercial.  While biggest rival Pixar is often limited by what can be accomplished within the confines of a G rating, DW seems increasingly proficient at packing as much into their PG pictures as possible.  There is no confusing the fact that this is an adult-oriented comedy that children will enjoy too (not the other way around).<br /><br />Considering the past two DreamWorks releases were How to Train Your Dragon and MegaMind, it's clear that Disney/ Pixar has their proverbial hands full.  Megamind may not be quite as butter-smooth, adventurous, or as grand-scoped as How to Train Your Dragon but it does harken back to the type of pacing and comedic brilliance viewers have come to expect from films like Kung-Fu Panda. All I knew was that for whatever reason, a lot of people seemed to like it, and now I know why. Will Grayson, Will Grayson is at once smart, witty, and emotional, with probably the best voice that I've read in a long time. I got ahold of this book by way of my local public library, but I'm tempted to buy it or, remarkably, take the time to write down some of its zingers so I can continue to laugh at them for months to come. Seriously, how can you read `My search party is creeping into your no man's land,' and not at least crack a smile? It cannot be done.<br /><br />This novel is the story of two teenage boys living in suburban Chicago who share the same first and last name. Due to a stunning coincidence one rainy night, the two of them bump into each other at an adult sex shop (no, I'm not kidding) and strike up an honest conversation on the sidewalk outside that leads to something bigger than either of them can comprehend.<br /><br />One thing I love about this book in particular is its inclusion of the LGBT community. Will Grayson's best friend (honestly I have no clue how to differentiate in a review which Will I'm talking about, so I'll just call this particular Grayson Will #1), Tiny Cooper, is about as big and fabulously gay as they come, and often drags Will #1 along for his Gay Straight Alliance meetings. Will #2 just happens to be gay as well (though not nearly as fabulous about it). The focus on homosexuality in this book is significant, but not overtly so. Never did I get the feeling that Green or Levithan was trying to be overly politically correct or offensive - the tone surrounding it was very easygoing. Yes, Tiny Cooper is very gay, but the writing makes him and other homosexuals out as `guys that happen to be gay' instead of `those flighty fairies.' This is the first YA book I've read where LGBT teens take center stage, so that's nice to see.<br /><br />If I had finished this review immediately upon finishing the book, the star rating would have been a bit higher. A few weeks later, some of the plot points and characterizations in the novel seem...for lack of a better word, schmultzy. For example, Tiny's play. What person is so full of himself to write a play entirely about his own love life? What's more, who is so full of himself to write a play entirely about his own love life...and then present it to the school board as something everyone would want to see? I mean seriously, Tiny names one of his characters Gill Wrayson. I get that in the end it's supposed to be about love and tolerance and all that, but if I saw that poster in my high school hallway, I'd be just as incredulous as I am now. The narration began to lose me in the second half, as well - I felt Green and Levithan were trying to sneak in way more introspection and philosophical musings than would be normal for a group of teenagers. Still, you gotta love when guys share man hugs and cry.o that's nice to see.    If I had finished this review immediately upon finishing the book, the star rating would have been a bit higher. 8186336141|5.0|This private label book was first published in 1966, well before the current yoga renaissance. Given the high level of interest in yoga therapy, it is astounding how far-sighted and prescient this book has been.    If we had to recommend just one book on yoga, this would be it. The title is a concise and precise summation of its contents. The book is comprehensive, free of ideology, and clinical in its writing. Even though it covers what seem like all yoga asanas, pranayamas, mudras, and bandhas, it is not overwhelming. This is because it is systematically organized into beginner, intermediate, and advanced groups of asanas, with step-by-step instructions as well as benefits and limitations or contraindications. Pranayama, bandha, and mudra follow the same level of detail as do the cleansing shat karma kriyas and a general introduction to chakras.    What makes this book particularly special is its enormously useful therapeutic index. For decades, this was the one book that listed not just the benefits, but also the contraindications of each asana. With increasing numbers of yoga injuries, and yoga-related ER visits, this information is something all yoga teachers as well as students should find helpful. Even after 40 years, the book remains unmatched.

That said, he is also often a good rapper.    And with regards to the music, their song-writing is formulaic, predictable and often lazy. There are so many filler moments where the music in the background just plays a bass line on repeat and Zack raps on and on. Almost all songs follow the same pattern; intro (the chorus backing music)/ quiet verse / heavy chorus /quiet verse / heavy chorus /instrumental / heavy chorus / some screaming (e.g. on the song Wake Up). Their songwriting is not very deep, the riffs can cross the line between catchy and repetitive. Repeat plays wear this album thin fairly quickly.     But their music is quite energetic, the guitarist Tom Morello has some very excellent work, he is very creative, especially with intriguing high-pitched effects. And these guys have some seriously rocking riffing. Like funk-rock that just explodes from your speakers or earphones, just like a bomb going off. And, take you hat off to Zack, he puts a lot of energy in his vocals.     Unfortunately, this album is too laborious to deserve a good rating. But at least they have a lot of talent and promise. Rating **

0793542049|4.0|I purchased this book based on the strength of the reviews. I was looking for a book to help learn how to play the bass, and to strengthen my understanding of what notes are where on the neck of the bass.<br /><br />The book stands as an excellent resource for learning how to construct bass lines over chord changes found in Jazz and similar styles. It begins simple, teaching you about finding and playing the root of the chord, and the fifth and octave. As you advance through the book, you begin to learn variations on these - different methods of approaching the root and fifth, as well as scales and arpeggios. Some sections explicitly lay out the notes to play, and more advanced sections simply give you the chord changes so that you can apply what you've learned in the prior lesson.<br /><br />Friedland also uses the book as a sort of crash course in music theory. In this, I believe the book suffers some. There is a lone page dedication to chord building, showing most of the variations of a C chord (major, minor, diminished, augmented, and the 7th variations of those). This is the only place where the chords are explicitly spelled out, if you aren't familiar enough with the notes in a given key to build a chord on your own, you might have a rough time of it. If you have a list of the key signatures in front of you, this weakness can be nullified or worked around. Obviously, if you have enough musical background to build the chords on your own, this isn't an issue at all. One of the negative reviewers comments that all of the examples are in C - if he only meant the \\"Basics of Chord Construction\\" section, then it's true, otherwise Friedland meanders across a wide variety of chords and keys.<br /><br />If you are looking for a book with tablature, don't look here. Some might find this a weakness, but I believe it to be a strength. In order to really understand how to construct your own bass lines, and to give yourself the freedom to move around the neck, being able toread music and understand the chords beyond simple numbers on a line is essential. I don't mind tabs, but I prefer to read the more descriptive standard notation as much as possible.<br /><br />All in all, a strong book, especially if you can construct chord spellings (or are capable of finding how yourself).

B003L20ID8|4.0|Based on a  Philip K. Dick short story, The Adjustment Bureau has romance and action going for it, with a touch of comedy and philosophy, too. It's science fiction, but unlike the futuristic Blade Runner, Total Recall, and Minority Report (also films based on Dick's works), it's set in the present.<br /><br />Basically, the film poses this question: Can free will, determination, and love triumph over determinism? Matt Damon is a young politician on the rise, who is programmed to do well with the public, with everything he does selected by consultants, who run things by focus groups--right down to which tie he wears. Emily Blunt (who plays a dancer) is there to inspire him to make a great concession speech, and then is supposed to vanish from his life, but the Adjustment Bureau (charged with making sure the right events occur, with the right people in power) screws up, and they fall in love.<br /><br />Damon and Blunt are terrific as the romantic leads, and Terence Stamp, Anthony Mackie, and John Slattery (all of whom must wear fedoras, which allow them to get through special doors to do their adjustment work quickly in New York City, in which the film takes place) are good in supporting roles.<br /><br />This is an entertaining and moving film, which somehow imparts enjoyment without the usual explosions and long car chases, although there are a couple of scenes which drag on a bit. All in all, I recommend this film for its romance, action sequences, good acting, and decent direction.

0316098337|3.0|Each year, a new Booker prize award committee announces its titles shortlist amidst great fanfare and invariably proclaiming it as the strongest set in years. As many of us readers would have found out, that kind of assessment is as objective and helpful as that of a recording artiste heralding each new release as his strongest album ever. The increasing incidence of duds appearing in shortlists has sadly discredited the judgment of experts who either have very different taste from the reading public or are simply way off in their selection. This year's Booker prize winner, \\"The Finkler Question\\" has attracted so many negative reviews from readers I cannot quite summon up my usual enthusiasm as I begin my trawl through the 2010 shortlist.     Emma Donoghue's \\"Room\\" seemed a safe bet. Sadly, I was never more irritated and frustrated reading the book, which I wanted very much to like but couldn't wait to finish.     The premise for the book - the story of a small boy, Jack, growing up captive in isolation with his mum (Ma) but for the occasional visitation of their captor Old Nick - is arguably novel and though likely inspired by true events isn't in my opinion remotely exploitative as some others have suggested. It wouldn't be the first time the narrative is voiced by a small child - remember Mark Haddon's \\"The Curious Incident Of The Dog In The Night Time\\" ? - but there's a special purpose to it in \\"Room\\". The story has to be told from the vantage perspective of somebody whose world and reality is defined exclusively by his restricted surroundings. So it isn't a gimmick - it wouldn't work for instance to have his mother tell the story. Remember, she's been out there before, though a long time ago.    The book can be divided into two parts - \\"before\\" and \\"after\\" escape. The \\"before\\" segment is better with more thrilling bits. The \\"after\\" segment dealing with the coping process has its share of drama but is comparatively flat.  But what surprised me most of all was that I couldn't get past even the first few pages of the book. I found myself stopping and starting as I wrestled painfully with the incoherent babble and self conscious baby talk, the quick succession toggling between dialogue and inner monologue, often reading and re-reading whole chunks of mother and son dialogue to make sense of it until I finally gave up. I figured, what they're on about isn't very interesting anyway so I sped read through most of it except when Old Nick made his appearances. Odd, but I liked it more when Old Nick was around.       In all fairness, the book has its merits. Donoghue nailed Ma's post-escape frustration with her own mother and the outside world right in the centre. They seemed to judge her like she was one of them, forgetting that Jack was her world during her captivity years (eg, like why was she was still breast feeding Jack at his age ?). But what finally blew it for me was Donoghue's decidedly shaky grasp of Jack's narrative voice - one moment sounding like a small boy or toddler even (using adjectives like \\"strong\\" as a noun, etc) and the next moment, making utterances or enunciating thoughts that only adults could have. This kind of failure is fatal to a book with such ambition - it makes it less believable.     Undoubtedly, many readers have enjoyed \\"Room\\". I know I didn't but I have no regrets reading it. At least I know why it didn't work for me.

B004FQKY5Q|5.0|The idea of bands writing and performing their own material has become a lost art these days. It seems as if every single pop act out there is pre-packaged, drenched with auto-tune, and overseen by the likes of Max Martin, Tricky Stewart, and Dr. Luke. To their credit, Per Gessle and Marie Fredrickson never needed that, because Gessle has a knack for constructing perfect little pop tunes. And with Fredrickson's unmistakable voice, the duo has left us with numerous pop classics that stick in the head for days, weeks, even years after you listen to them.<br /><br />Roxette has never received it's proper due, however, because they are dismissed by some critics as merely a guilty pleasure. But the duo takes a step back from that on this record, producing their most organic set of power-pop tunes yet. Some of the synths and electronic elements remain (\\"Big Black Cadillac\\"), but even on the songs that are fun and upbeat (\\"Way Out\\") the blips and blats give way to strumming guitars and layered vocals. The drums are turned up in the mix, and the vocals shine. Gessle is in the finest voice he's ever been in, and Fredrickson is her usual, reliable self with her amazing voice.<br /><br />Of the twelve songs, there are a few clunkers, notably the slower ballads (which oddly was always their forte). But when they are on, Roxette produces some of their best work. \\"Dream On\\" is one of the best songs Gessle ever wrote, and \\"She's Got Nothing On (But the Radio)\\" is an instant pop classic. Overall, this is a nice little gem of an album by two underrated musicians who many might have considered past their prime.

0756404738|5.0|Normally I wouldn't wait four years for a book. Usually after the first couple years I give up on the author, especially if it's part of a projected series or trilogy (yes I mean you GRRM!). Never has a fantasy story come off as charming and engaging and despite my doubts over the long development time I was more than willing to give the sequel a shot. Now 'Wise Man's Fear' is finally out (the second in the projected 'Kingkiller Chronicles' trilogy) and while it's a bit longer than it's predecessor I can say that the four year wait was more than worth it!<br /><br />'Wise Man's Fear' picks up directly after the events of 'Name of the Wind'. Kvothe is a legendary figure who's presumed dead and running a small town Inn. Over the course of 3 days Kvothe is retelling his life-story to a chronicler who knows his real identity. In his story (the book's story within a story) the young Kvothe is still at the University from the first novel and is still butting heads with Ambrose, a rival student. After being forced into leaving the University Kvothe travels to Severen where he gets caught up in courtly politics and uncovers an assassination attempt. He also helps mercenary warriors battle bandits, trains under the mysterious Adem and ends up having his legendary meeting with the Felurian. Adding to this Kvothe develops his charming (if predictable) relationship with the sly but beautiful Denna and continues his vengeful search for the Chandrian: the creatures who murdered his parents when he was a child.<br /><br />Needless to say this 1000+ page book covers a lot of ground. By the end I was left wanting as there were still a lot of unresolved threads (even more so than 'Name of the Wind') but I was never once disappointed. This book is a must read and while new readers should definitely start with 'Name of the Wind', know 'Wise Man's Fear' is just as strong and engagingst as, if not more, leisurely paced than 'Name of the Wind' and can sometimes be a bit too self-indulgent (and long-winded). 0689865384|4.0|As a middle school student who \\"studied\\" Uglies by Scott Westerfeld at school, and one who enjoys such science fiction books, I can tell you that this book is one surely to be enjoyed by middle-schoolers, or any adult who likes science fiction.   This book, Uglies, put in the setting of the future, the world of Tally Youngblood is nothing like it is here, in \\"our world.\\" 2011 has passed millions of years ago, and we, us in 2011 are called the \\"rusties,\\" known for our crazy acts of hideous things like pollution, cutting down trees, and killing animals. Something they wouldn't even think of doing.   In a society of four classes--littlies, uglies, pretties, and specials--well, the name explains it. Up until you're 12, you're a littlie, then when you turn twelve--you live in the dorms of uglies, and when you turn sixteen, you get an operation that changes you--inside out. They rub off your skin, give you new, soft skin, big vulnerable eyes, perfect teeth, nose--everything, almost like being born again, as a new appearance, perfect, pretty. When you're a pretty, your only job is to have fun and party.   To any ugly, being pretty and partying all day seems like the best thing to do--but is it?  As Tally finds a friend who decides to run away from becoming pretty and join a \\"juvenile\\" group of kids and adult uglies who have escaped the operation into turning pretty, Tally must face betrayal, self conflict, and finding the truth to being pretty.  If I were to rate this book, I'd rate it a four. Not a five--that's reserved for the best of best books, and Uglies isn't what you'd call the best of best--it's the ending and structure that ruined my thought of it.   I didn't like simplicity of the book, of the plot. It was basically like almost any other science fiction book that you would find--in the future, in a society of different people, running away, protesting, etc. Many books I read are very similar to it. Another thing was the whole series. Uglies is merely the introduction. Following Uglies is Pretties, Specials, then Extras. Uglies I liked because it was the first book, and although it was similar to the others, it still had a taste to it because it, at least, had different details and plot. However, the following books were too predictable, too similar to the first book, almost the same format as Uglies, and I found myself in a guessing game with \\"I bet this is going to happen next\\" and the titles gave the away, too (if you haven't read it, you wouldn't understand). Therefore, I found Pretties and Specials boring and tedious. However, the little details and \\"mini-plots\\" were what were more interesting--which is the mere reason I continued to read (and the curiosity of what the ending would be like). I liked Extras, because it was quite different than the prior three books of the series, but it was pretty much the same thing--except instead of everyone wanting to be pretty, everyone wanted to be popular--pretty much coming across the same idea that I said before, if everyone's pretty, then is anyone pretty?  I find this book would be hard to understand for younger readers in elementary schools, no matter how easy the words itself are. The meaning behind a book is what is most important, to my opinion, which, therefore, concludes to my suggestion that this book should be mainly for people at the age 11 and up. You really need to listen to the hidden messages of the metaphors and the \\"behind the lines\\" meaning to not just read this book as \\"any other science fiction book.\\"  Of course, I have read this once on my own, and once again at school. One would understand that in school, you go more \\"in depth\\" of the book and \\"dissect\\" it more than you would if you were just reading it for fun. The book is a good read, full of metaphors that you can try to understand, and it is such a clever plot, how everything weaves together to form that one last ending. There was one \\"moral,\\" you could call it, that formed around the book, and if you paid enough attention to the story and \\"behind the scenes,\\" as some often say, you would realize that it also came across a very important point that applies to us today. Not just \\"don't pollute,\\" but the thought of, if everyone's pretty, then is anyone pretty? The standard of normal changes from `ugly' to `pretty,' so if everyone's pretty, being pretty is being normal--nothing special after all, nothing special about you being pretty. The book gave a meaning to the reader that being \\"like everyone else\\" isn't always the best idea, which is why this book suits middle-schoolers more than adults, when they are in the midst of getting independent and thinking of things themselves. As a quote on some book covers of Uglies suggests, \\"In a world of extreme beauty, anyone normal is ugly.\\" Tally isn't appreciating herself as herself. Being compared to those with extreme beauty isn't the best way to live, and to Tally, she and other uglies are hideous, and need the operation to become pretty. To us, this tells us not to be depressed that we are \\"different,\\" because being different is not bad at all, and in some ways, is better than being like \\"everyone else.\\"  After reading this book, I thought again about how our world is right now, and how we can improve it. It made me think to appreciate myself as how I am, and also that being \\"like everyone else\\" isn't always the best thing.

0393061310|5.0|This was a fascinating read. It provides terrific info about and understandings of the history of us humans. However, its explanations, based largely on geography and natural resources, are limited. While these factors undoubtedly affected the history of peoples in different regions of the world, they don't explain why China took off economically in the late 70s and Africa is just beginning to find its economic feet. Why did the colonies of England (U.S., Australia, Canada, N.Z.) mostly recover and thrive after conquest while the colonies of other European countries mostly failed to? The author, Jared Diamond, over-estimates the effects of geography and natural resources while under-estimating the effects of culture on historical outcomes. I don't expect a book to cover all factors within its subject, but I do expect it to provide balanced understanding of how important various factors might be. The author's view of geography as deterministic of outcomes should be challenged; there's a harmfulness to maintaining that humans are hapless victims of their environment. Thankfully, there are books like \\"A Farewell to Alms\\" that provide a different view. At the same time, Jared Diamond has made a real contribution, if biased, to understanding human history. No question that it's an interesting and important book.

B000002MFQ|4.0|At the time of its release in 1992, Mike Oldfield suggested that this album could be considered the first ever music sequel.  The word sequel is a  loaded one, connoting Hollywood's particular brand of cynicism  along with an implied lack of originality and creativity.  But sequel is a good word to describe Tubular Bells 2.  It takes the  overall structure of 1973's Tubular Bells and uses it as a template to construct a new album whose melodies are related - at times very closely - to those of the original. It is a interesting idea and perhaps the only time it has ever been attempted. The album doesn't feel like an exercise in cynicism - not in the way later sequels, remakes and rebrandings would.  It comes across as a genuine experiment, one undertaken with real enthusiasm,  although in titling it Tubular Bells 2, Oldfield was certainly guaranteeing himself more publicity and greater sales than he had been experiencing in the years prior to 1992.       So strongly is Tubular Bells 2 a sequel to Tubular Bells that it is almost impossible to regard it as a standalone album - by using the original as a constant referencing point Tubular Bells 2  forces one to examine it in light of its predecessor. So how does it hold up against its beloved parent record? Well, for a start it's a much lighter, sweeter album than Tubular Bells. Mike's dark emotional state during the creation and recording of Tubular Bells is well documented. By 1992, he had left Virgin, a company he felt had little interest in his work. He was signed to a new label and his new found positivity comes through on this record clearly.  But the emotionality of the original is not often present on Tubular Bells 2. Some of this lies in the production, courtesy of Trevor Horn, who helped give this album a thickly reverberant and very processed sound which at times feels antiseptic.  The dark and often harsh sounds of the original's organs  and keyboards have here been replaced by synthesizer patches, sequenced guitars and wordless human voices which function as pad sounds - thoroughly inoffensive but rather dull.  Anything hard, sharp or abrasive has been carefully filled down and rounded off and the album suffers for this. The rawness and dirtiness of the original gave it an immediacy which is part of the reason why it spoke ( and continues to speak) to so many people.       Tubular Bells 2 is organised into discrete tracks, although they flow into each other, effectively arranging the album into a \\"Side One\\"  and a Side Two\\".  At its best, the album's production becomes an asset. In Weightless , which opens  \\"Side Two\\", there are lots of small sounds rolling around, all beautifully placed in the mix; the voices are unobtrusive and the sequenced guitars bring a clean hypnotic quality. Sentinel achieves a similar effect, channeling the famous opening piano motif of Tubular Bells very nicely. Then there are sparse sections such as Blue Saloon,  where Tubular Bells 2 captures some of the more sinister qualities of the original. And Mike can still turn an exquisitely pretty melody, as he does in Great Plain, which uses mandolins and a banjo to wonderful effect. After two minutes the track becomes a bouncy and sequenced variation of itself which has its own charms, although such moments tend to use synthesizer patches which hold the album firmly in the year of its creation.      Unfortunately the Bell, the climactic section of  what is effectively \\"Side one\\",  is nowhere near as good as the corresponding part of the original. The build up to the main melody is far shorter, to the point of feeling cursory, and the melody itself is not nearly as memorable.  Alan Rickman, as the Master of Ceremonies, is theatrical without being melodramatic, but the whole section is played too much for comedy (\\"Two Slightly Sampled Electric Guitars\\") which strains repeated listening.  In  \\"Side Two\\" we have Altered state, the low point of the album. The drums are pedestrian, both in executionand in sound. The female voices are horribly unfunny, pulling you out of the listening experience,  and the Piltdown man who was so distinctive in Tubular Bells, here sounds like a bad Chewbacca impersonator.  It's a shame, because when the voices are silent, the track improves greatly, with some wonderful guitar work from Mike.      Overall, it's an interesting album, only partly successful. Later Mike would create more sequels to Tubular Bells. He would re-record it, re-mix it and use it's famous twisted bell as a logo on dubious projects such as The Millennium Bell, and in some of these cases the charge of cynicism is much harder to rebut.  But Tubular Bells 2 is a good album - not great - it's anodyne production alone would  keep it away from any claims to greatness. But it is genuine, often beautiful, far from cynical and certainly qualifies as an essential album for a Mike Oldfield fan.

The plot centers around a mythical 9th Roman Legion which supposedly marched into the wilderness of Britain to subjugate the Picts, a wild, indigenous group of \\"barbarians\\", and were never heard of again. The plot of the movie is entirely fictitious, created by writer-director Neil Marshall to explain the disappearance, and he succeeds beautifully with an intricate and fascinating story providing non-stop action centering around several key figures on both sides.    Dominic West, portraying a fierce Roman general, is the male standout, and the magnificent Olga Kurylenko as a mute and supernaturally competent Pict tracker is riveting. Michael Fassbender, the \\"hero\\" is excellent, but somewhat understated in comparison. His ability to survive repeated harrowing and potentially lethal encounters is the backbone of the movie, and is impressive without seeming too contrived.    The action scenes are suitably gory, with decapitations and blood spray worthy of Spartacus Blood and Sand, and in an unusual concession to reality, people who get injured....stay injured, and do not magically appear in perfect health in the next scene. When frail Olga's character destroys big, powerful men in hand-to-hand combat, it does strain credibility just a bit, but I have come to appreciate strong female action characters far more than the boring old convention that women in action and horror movies are present simply to be helpless, saved by men, and to scream a lot.    In conclusion, speaking as a fan of this genre, with fairly high standards and absolutely no patience for mundane garbage, I highly recommend this movie. It is well conceived, beautifully photographed, skillfully acted, fast paced, and very, very entertaining.

B004DTLK8A|4.0|\\"Elysium\\" is the 13th brand new studio album by Finnish power metal band \\"Stratovarius\\" and the second to feature guitarist Matias Kupiainen replacing founder Timmo Tolkki. Although the cover references directly the artistic vision of the previous album \\"Polaris\\" (with the starship having landed), the album follows a somewhat different musical approach than the established power metal sound of `Stratovarius'.  Specifically the tempo has been dropped considerably from the speeding rhythm of \\"Polaris\\" to a more standard tempo, with the band clearly prioritizing choruses and melodies over speed. There is also evidence of a minor shift toward a neoclassical sound referencing to a small extent the band's early 90's style.<br /><br />The guitarist's contribution is once again key, having co-written six out of nine overall songs on the album; It would be fair to say that the newcomer Kupiainen and singer Timmo Kotipelto have developed into the main songwriters of \\"Stratovarius\\" being their collective effort that brought most of the songs on \\"Elysium\\".  Jens Johansson also contributes two songs he wrote single handedly.<br /><br />One has to also mention that the drop in speed makes most of the material on \\"Elysium\\" dramatic and more passionate than expected, with standouts \\"Infernal Maze\\", \\"Lifetime in a moment\\" and \\"Move the mountain\\".  Overall the new direction is probably refreshing to the melodic metal style of \\"Stratovarius\\" as it once again brings to front memorable tunes, although \\"Elysium\\" should have featured more than nine songs.

1416550534|4.0|I read Kate Morton's The Forgotten Garden and it was so good, I simply had to read the first book she wrote, The House at Riverton. I was not disappointed. It took me about 100 pages to really feel pulled into the story but that's not uncommon with large tales that span a lifetime. A 90 year old woman's memories just prior to her death, span her servitude from the age of 14 to approximately 28, to the aristocratic family living at the house of Riverton. The story shows the incredible sense of duty and loyalty servants were bred to give their masters/mistresses and their children. It's a tragedy set in a socio-historical period when women weren't encouraged to read, have opinions or be intelligent. Repressed emotion and unfulfilled dreams were the order of the day. One could say this period of history around WWI was the antithesis to self-actualization. What a sad state of affairs. I have to say the most fascinating aspect of this novel was the period in which it was set. Morton paints a picture of what life was like for the servants and the leisure class, revealing both to have unfulfilled lives based on society's rigid expectations. Aristocracy was all about safe-guarding the family name and inheritance, while servants were to act like shadows to those they waited upon, guessing their every need while denying their own. Who can be happy in that picture? You guessed it. The stage is set for tragedy upon tragedy. I found I did not care for the characters the way I wanted to. The hero of the novel, for me, turned out to be Alfred, a soldier returned from war who has the courage to break free from servitude and build a life of his own. The women were sad products of their time, struggling to live true to their needs, desires and dreams, but not quite getting there. I would say Grace moved on solely by virtue of circumstance, not inner resolve. My other criticism is the character of Frederick is most annoying. We never quite understand why he is so silly with his children. Not talking to them because he didn't care for their decisions only rendered him selfish in my eyes. Moping about the grounds day after day almost turned him into a sort of comedic character who I hoped would just go away. It was impossible for me to feel empathy for him. Sure he was in his late brother's shadow and his business failed. I guess the moral of that story is, he who has no freedom to be who he is, cultivates no inner resources?     However, overall, the story is magnificently told, and if you read The Forgotten Garden, you will see in both books, Kate Morton is a fabulous writer. Nuances dot her story lines as she mirrors Grace's motivations and choices to that of Hannah's, and the house at Riverton's deterioration and later false portrayal through tourism, to society's desolation and then drunken gaiety after WWI. I wasn't into opera at that time and he said he would convert me and after heariing hearing Il Trovatore I was hooked and I couldn't get enough of it. My next set was Wagner's Siegfried,(eventually the rest of the Ring) do you mind. I was off into the deep end no turning back now!<br />I had never know of a French version before now, so I tracked it down to an Italian version with the French ending.<br />The variation in the endings is as follows: In the original Italian version; Manrico is beheaded by the axe and in the French version he is sent to the gallows. Verdi quite rightly realized that there was not enough music for the extra time required for this style of execution; as there are only 11 bars of music between the moment when Manrico is dragged away and the Conte says \\"E spento!\\" (He is Dead\\") this being enough time to kill someone by axe. But for the gallows, this required more time and music. So what he did to extend the sequence, was to revamp the \\"Miserere\\" from the preceding scene (Leonora and Monks: Part Four Scene One) as a trio for Manrico, the Conte and Azucena with the monks choir off in the distance. The reason for choosing this piece was because of its solemn \\"andante sostenuto\\" which contrasts with the previous allegro section, then concludes with the original final bars. This makes sense of the time line and has a much more dramatic conclusion to the opera. This when premiered at the Paris Opera was sung in French. Sadly there is no French version recorded to date other that this Italian translated one. I actually prefer this ending. The cast is exceptional as well: The great Dutch nightingale Cristina Deutekom is Leonora; Juan Lloveras is Manrico; Jan Derksen is Il Conte di Luna; Carolyne James is Azucina; Tom Haenen is Ferrando; Renee van Haarlem is Ines and Adriaan van Limpt is Ruiz with the Nederlands Operakoos and the Utrechts Symfonieorkest conducted by Christopher Keene on Gala (Live Recording). Good sound quality along with the obligatory Coughs and Splutters for good measures. Il Trovatore is the opera I use to introduce opera novices to the wonderful world of opera as is the only opera which has 26 hummable tunes in it.<br />If you love Verdi; if you love Il Trovatore; buy it and relive a magical opera.

0875845851|5.0|Why have many once market-leading companies failed to stay relevant? It would be easy to assume that they had stagnant engineers or complacent management, but Clayton Christensen concludes otherwise: \\"Because they carefully studied market trends and systematically allocated investment capital to innovations that promised the best returns, they lost their positions of leadership.\\"    How is that possible? The key is to understand the distinction between sustaining and disruptive innovation.  Large companies are good at sustaining innovation -- product improvements demanded by existing customers.     Disruptive technologies do not serve mainstream markets initially. In fact, the market may not be obvious at all.  Because there is no existing market, there is no reliable market research.  That's the innovator's dilemma.     Disruptive technologies are generally brought to market by start-ups.  They find their niche through trial and error.  Initially their functionality doesn't satisfy the mainstream, but with successive generations this problem is overcome, displacing the old technology.     Christensen concentrated on the disk drive industry because new generations of technology are released frequently.  However, the principles of disruptive technology are not limited to high technology. He also presents examples from the excavation equipment, steel, motorcycle, and retail industries.     This is an insightful, substantive book. The most notable weakness is the graphics.  The purpose of graphs is to present data in a visual form that is easy to digest.  Some of the graphs in this book add more confusion than clarity.

The originals, let's face it, were overproduced, mainly because John was feeling particularly insecure about his voice. So these four are, with appropriate fades implemented, MUCH preferable. The `Stripped Down' `Dear Yoko' is also better, but it's still not as good as the version on `The Lennon Anthology', which Lennon sings with abandon. As for `Woman' and `Watching the Wheels', I think the gloss on the originals was warranted and makes those the better versions.  As for Yoko's stuff, I didn't listen to it, mainly because I never had a problem with her original versions. Plus, Yoko and John are for the most part a mismatch on record. One is better off alternating the best of Yoko's tracks with the best of step-son Julian or son Sean--as per the bonus CDs suggested in the book cited above. This is no slight to Yoko. I believe that Yoko at her best is one of the more formidable female pop artists of the past 40 years. Granted she's an acquired taste, but for Beatles/John fans, it's a (selective) taste worth acquiring.  Three decades is a heck of a long interval, but thank God that Yoko and Jack Douglas buried the hatchet and got this project done. Four greatly-improved tracks justify their efforts.

People always compare and contrast these two movies because one was the original that blazed the trail while the second one has its own unique identity.  The 1931 version is in black and white, has slow pacing, no musical score, and has a dark and creepy atmosphere throughout.  In contrast, the 1958 movie is in color, has much faster pacing, a dramatic and bombastic musical score, and has a much brighter atmosphere.  Of course, the inevitable comparison is always made between Bela Lugosi's portrayal of Dracula vs. Christopher Lee's portrayal.  You be the judge.<br /><br />Now it's time to review Horror Of Dracula (1958), starring Peter Cushing as Van Helsing, Christopher Lee as Dracula, Michael Gough as Arthur, Carol Marsh as Lucy, Melissa Stribling as Mina, and John Van Eyssen as Jonathan Harker.  This movie is only 81 minutes long.  I feel that it's a little too short for a movie based on Bram Stoker's novel.  A movie based on Stoker's novel needs to be a little longer because there is such a large amount of material to cover.  I don't think it should be 2 1/2 hours long like the 1977 BBC miniseries Count Dracula (which is TOO long), but Horror Of Dracula could have benefited from an extra 20 minutes or more so the suspense can build and more character development can take place.  The movie is too condensed into 81 minutes.  I do think, however, that it's kind of hard to make a movie based on Stoker's novel to begin with because the slower parts of the novel pretty much force changes to be made to the plot, characters, etc.  In order to include everything in the novel, including the boring stuff, the movie needs to be a 2 1/2 hour BBC miniseries like Count Dracula.  The result is a movie that drags.  If you want a movie with faster pacing, you have to cut out a lot from the novel and make other changes.  The pacing problem is solved, but Horror Of Dracula is TOO short.  Movies such as Dracula (1979), which is 110 minutes, and Bram Stoker's Dracula (1992), which is 130 minutes, are just right.<br /><br />I think Christopher Lee is a good Dracula. The Quadrilogy is out of production, and may have lost some of its luster, but it is still a fantastic collection, and can be found used, at very reasonable prices, as Alien fans look to upgrade to the Blu-ray set.  The Quadrilogy is a great buy for casual fans who may not have all of these films.  The bonus material is prodigious, highly informative, and helps place these films in historical context.  About the only disappointment is that there is very little heard from David Fincher, the director of Alien 3.  Unfortunately, Fincher's side of the story regarding the making of Alien 3, has to this day, yet to be adequately told.<br /><br />A true classic in the realm of science fiction, the Alien films are each distinct, and featured directors who at the time were relatively unheralded. However, the story pacing is wrong. Having read Rothfuss' blog and how he painstakingly edited down the size of the book over the years, I was surprised at the end result. There is a bandit hunt that could have been cut down to half the size (really, we didn't need to hear details like the supply runs rotations). The time spent with Felurian should have been cut down as well. The Adem were slightly more interesting, but I got the feeling that everything went a bit too easily for Kvothe.    All in all a decent read, but like another previewer mentions, if you completely skip this book before reading part three you probably won't be too lost. Kvothe learned about sex, a bit of fighting and he got a new cloak. When you can resume a whole book in a single 14 words sentence without forgetting anything important, there's a problem. There's a little bit more groundwork laid down about the main storyline, but precious few of it.    That said, it's still a good read if you enjoyed the first book. You just aren't likely to be blown away with anything in the second volume.

Sarah has her own particular horror to live through that involves her young brother Michel, who gets left behind when the family is arrested.<br /><br />Meanwhile, in modern-day Paris, Julia Jarmond, a middle-aged American now living in Paris (and married to a Frenchman) is set to move into the apartment where Sarah's family lived in 1942. Julia discovers this connection when she begins researching the \\"Vel Dhiv\\" roundup for a newspaper article she's writing and then, when she learns that her husband's family took the apartment when a Jewish family was arrested, becomes obsessed with finding out what happened to that family, which eventually leads her to Sarah's trail. However, I still had to give this book a five. The major conflicts were not resolved, but they were deepened, made more real, and the tension was raised even higher. Kvothe is neither deified by an adoring public nor shunned as a pariah, but he grows as a person and becomes, if not more relatable, more understandable. While the antagonists are not soundly vanquished, new conflicts enter in that more than make up for it. This is a middle book, and it shows, but that doesn't make this anything less than a stellar read.

0756404738|2.0|Please note: There is a section with spoilers marked with ***. Feel free to skip if you'd like.<br /><br />Let me preface my review by saying I loved Name of the Wind.<br /><br />Love, love, LOVED it.<br /><br />Name of the Wind is without a doubt the best fantasy novel I've read in the past 15 years (*Edit: That distinction now belongs to Anthony Ryan's Raven Shadow Book I: Blood Song. I highly suggest you check it out.*).  The personal, gripping, intimate nature of getting inside Kvothe's head was a true joy in Kingkiller Book I.<br /><br />As a result, my hopes were incredibly high for the sequel, maybe a little too high. And don't get me wrong, being with Kvothe again was for a time enjoyable, like putting on a comfortable pair of jeans you haven't worn for a while. Believe me, nothing would have pleased me more than for Wise Man's Fear to be a bravura, 5-star outing for Rothfuss.<br /><br />But as much as I wanted to undyingly, unabashedly love this book I just ... couldn't. Rothfuss' genius with prose and demonstrable wit remain intact, but due to some inexplicable plot and character structuring, the Wise Man's Fear simply falls flat. Even worse, however, it subverts our ability to enjoy the epic Story of Kvothe at all.<br /><br />When it's all said and done, a three-word review of Wise Man's Fear could be put down thusly:<br /><br />\\"Huh? What the...?\\"<br /><br />That, in a nutshell, is the experience of reading the second novel of the Kingkiller Chronicles. Though occasionally exhilarating and intriguing, too often The Wise Man's Fear simply leaves you scratching your head. As readers it feels as if we're no longer \\"living\\" in the world Rothfuss creates; instead we're \\"peeking behind the curtain,\\" watching the author pull the strings--\\"Oooh, look how cool this story is! Isn't this story neat? Kvothe's amazing, isn't he amazing?\\"<br /><br />Other reviewers have complained that the biggest problem with Wise Man's Fear is that \\"nothing happens\\" in terms of the\\"big picture\\" of the story. That accusation is accurate, but only a symptom of the broader, overarching problem: Rothfuss' \\"vision for the narrative\\" now overrides the need for a coherent, engaging plot and believable character motivations. The result is not unlike watching a film by a famous director run amok, indulging in personal whims because they know their studio / editor won't get in the way.<br /><br />This sensibility from Rothfuss is baffling, because the Name of the Wind had virtually none of it. If there are two words I would use to describe The Name of the Wind, they would be \\"immersive\\" and \\"organic.\\" From start to finish, you FEEL that you are a part of the world, watching \\"real\\" events happen within it.<br /><br />In Wise Man's Fear, on the other hand, the opposite is true--a pervasive, not-quite-unseen \\"contrived-ness\\" underlies almost everything.<br /><br />Too often Kvothe seems to do things because \\"the story\\" requires it, not because the character himself would be internally motivated to do so. Elements of his psychology are stripped away and tossed by the wayside, with barely an afterthought or explanation. As a result, our emotional connection with Kvothe wanes; we are far less, not more invested in our hero, increasingly ambivalent to whether he succeeds or fails. Kvothe is still \\"the Story,\\" but it's no longer clear whether he's a person or plot device--Kvothe the Character, or Kvothe the Deus Ex Machina.<br /><br />This is not to say that Wise Man's Fear has no redeeming qualities. Rothfuss's prose remains strong as ever: lyrical, subtle, intensely, lovingly crafted. Not all of the plot is wasted either; Kvothe's struggle to earn the respect of his peers, and for himself continues to resonate--when contextualized by the author. Rothfuss seems to forget that we like Kvothe not because he is super-human, but because he is altogether TOO human, and the scenes relating to that struggle--with Devi, Ambrose, the Maer, and up to a point, with Denna--continue to compel. It is in these scenes we come to see ourselves through Kvothe.<br /><br />Sadly, there's not nearly enough of them, and they come so sporadically that pacing and continuity, the book's sense of purpose, suffers. Too often we're asked as readers to suspend belief, plausibility, and authenticity so the author can \\"tell his story the way he wants it told.\\"<br /><br />*** SPOILERS HERE ***<br /><br />The most egregious offense to internal consistency is without question the Felurian sub-plot. Its sole purposes seem to be to heavy-handedly tell the reader, \\"Kvothe is now the most sexually experienced human being in the history of this, or any other world,\\" and to introduce Deus Ex Machina Extraordinaire (there's that phrase again) the Cthaeh. The problems with this section are numerous, and for an author of Rothfuss' stature and formidable talents, it's frankly an embarrassment.<br /><br />\\"Running off to the Faerie realm to discover the undiscoverable\\" is hardly a fresh fantasy trope, but since Rothfuss gives zero context before or after for what transpires, the entire instance feels superfluous, a waste of time. To make matters worse, the handling of the sexual content is eye-rollingly facile at best, and as others have commented, could be construed as outright offensive to women at worst. (As a side note, I just can't figure out Rothfuss's aversion to letting Kvothe remain nuanced. It wasn't enough for him to be a world-class mage, scholar, and musician, but now has to be world's greatest lover as well? Kvothe the Deus Ex, it seems, has to either be the \\"greatest ever,\\" or nothing at all.)<br /><br />\\"Surviving an encounter with Felurian\\" is supposed to be part of Kvothe's mystique, but as a reader it comes across as just straight-up bizarre (and not in a good way). But since Pat hinted at this whole Felurian thing on the cover sleeve of Book 1, um, well, guess we have to do it anyway, no matter how nonsensical and out of character it feels. From start to finish, it's a bad concept horribly executed, and to add insult to injury, it's not even really necessary. Both \\"Kvothe learns to get some\\" and the Cthaeh could have been thrown in just about anywhere--\\"Hey Bast, remember the time I banged Random Chick #77, and then blah blah blah and met the Cthaeh?\\"<br /><br />On the whole I'm a little more forgiving of the Ademre sub-plot--though it also dragged on too long--because at least it arose from the actual in-character development of Kvothe's friendship with Tempi. But even then, the sexual content feels more indulgent than internally motivated by the character, and the effects of Kvothe's time spent with the Ademre are inconsistent with what we see later, during his interactions with the fake Edemah Ruh troop and back at the University.<br /><br />*** END SPOILERS ***<br /><br />In the end, for all of Pat's talent, mind, heart, and wit, as readers we end up questioning his intentions. There's no substantial character growth, plot arc, or sense of how or why any of this actually MATTERS--to Kvothe, or to us.<br /><br />It's a supreme paradox, to see such well-crafted, delicious prose mixed so brutally with a meaningless plot and incoherent character structuring. As much as I love the author, and wanted to gloss over The Wise Man's Fear's significant issues, ultimately I realized that I was no longer invested in the fiction. I simply no longer BELIEVED what I was reading, and the world and Kvothe had ceased to be plausible or compelling.<br /><br />The end result for this reader has been similar to George Lucas' ill-conceived Star Wars prequels: The Wise Man's Fear now taints, rather than enhances, the entire Rothfuss experience.<br /><br />To be sure, if you're a Rothfuss fan, the enduring intrigue of the protagonist and the author's remarkable wit are enough to propel the book to an above-average read. But there's a part of me that thinks, no, KNOWS, that there's a better story in here than the one Pat put to paper. For good or ill, this is clearly the product Pat wanted us to have--and like movie director Baz Luhrmann at his worst, Rothfuss' excesses get in the way of the enjoyment, rather than add to it.

0786915749|2.0|This is one of the worst fantasy novels that I actually finished. Here's why:    DERIVATIVE:    I don't know what's all the hype and fuss about Raistlin. He's simply a version of Moorcock's ELRIC OF MELNIBONE--a character who predates Raistlin by about a decade. Both are gifted with amazing powers but are also plagued by physical limitations.     The scene where Tas manipulates the fake dragon reminded me of the movie THE WIZARD OF OZ.    The scene where the characters are transported cross-country by the pegasi is comparable to the scene where the Eagles transport characters in THE LORD OF THE RINGS and THE HOBBIT. Also comparable are the times that characters \\"die\\" only to be resurrected--but in this book characters \\"die\\" and come back in the short span of one chapter.    Bupu's command of language is on a level with TARZAN (Me Tarzan. You Jane).    In general, the whole book reads like a combination of bad pulp fiction and the script for an old HE-MAN cartoon.    RIDICULOUS NAMES:    These names speak for themselves . . .    VERMINAARD  BUPU  HIGHBULP PHUDGE I  FISTANDANTILUS  CATYRPELIUS    Seriously?!?!?    CHARACTER DEVELOPMENT:    The very first page in the paperback edition of this book shows a nice list of 8 characters, each with their own past histories and characteristics. I actually was looking forward to reading about some of these. Characters like Tanis had enormous potential.    Unfortunately, the further I read, the more I realized something:  The characters hardly ever change!  Tanis, Sturm, Goldmoon, Riverwind, Caramon, Flint and Tasslehoff--all these characters stay pretty much the same. We see a brief moment of compassion in Raistlin and a bit of a change in Laurana, but that's all.    So why is the character development so poor? Read on . . .    TOO MUCH ACTION:    If you break this novel down, it's basically 90% action and 10% everything else, which includes character development. So much action is taking place that the characters scarcely havea CHANCE to sit back, take it easy and reflect on what has happened and how it might have changed them and their relationship with the others. There virtually no introspection and I don't even recall the characters ever THINKING. All their actions are in plain view.    Furthermore, the MOTIVE for the Dark Queen wanting to take over the world is never really explained. But I guess with names like Dark Queen and Verminaard the authors felt like they didn't have to justify their motives--they're the bad guys, boo them and cheer the good guys. Reading this whole book I never had a sense of DIRECTION as to what the characters where trying to accomplish and more importantly, WHY?    OVERALL:    Even if we wound the clock back to 1984, there still isn't much to recommend this book. Back then I would rather be reading ROBERT E. HOWARD, HP LOVECRAFT, MICHAEL MOORCOCK, JRR TOLKIEN, GENE WOLFE and JOHN MYERS MYERS, to name a few.    But it's not 1984, it's 2011.   Do yourself a favor and read some of the above and then add GEORGE RR MARTIN, CHINA MIEVILLE, ROBERT JORDAN, GUY GAVRIEL KAY, BRANDON SANDERSON, JIM BUTCHER, TERRY PRATCHETT and many other authors that can write books that put this one to absolute shame.

B0042RJWTC|2.0|Was Henry VIII so quiet and unassuming, was his life so staid and predictable that U.S. audiences would never sit through a series based on his exploits? The people who brought us 'The Tudors' certainly thought so.  The Irish/Canadian production team took one look at Henry, his court, his wives, his wars and his battles with the Church--and his impact on world history, culture, art, literature, politics, religion, geography and music and said, \\"How the heck can we make this guy's life sound interesting?\\"<br /><br />'The Tudors' plays so fast and loose with history that even someone with my minimal knowledge had to constantly point at the screen and hoot.  But hey, it's TV, so let's not nitpick. The episodes are colorful and entertaining and we are, after all, just Americans.  Why give us content when all we want is 'shiny.'<br /><br />The series goes off the rails from the gitgo. Here's what one might learn from Season One:  The Boleyn family was apparently unaware of the existence of printed books (which had been introduced about one hundred years earlier). It took Cromwell to explain the concept of a printing press to a mystified George B.  Princess Mary, Henry's sister is combined with Lady Mary, his eldest daughter. The producers no doubt decided anyone with the monicker \\"Bloody Mary\\" was the money Mary. With Henry's penchant for marrying Catherines/Katherines, Annes and Janes, they must have assumed that we'd never handle a pair of Marys and decided to spare us the additional confusion. You have to respect that kind of consideration for viewers.<br /><br />The series shows Wolsey committing suicide in prison after being tried and found guilty of treason. Big deal. So the man died of natural causes before the trial, but, hey nonny nonny, it's close enough for madrigals.  And at least they don't have the poor Cardinal  doing an Isadora Duncan dance routine in his his cell -- which is how little Cathy Howard spends her last, precious moments.<br /><br />The people and the props seem to be plucked from a generic \\"Costume Drama: Days of Old When Knights Were Bold Period\\" warehouse.  The anachronisms are egregious and occur in scene after scene, episode after episode.  People travel in carriages, Henry fires a flintlock musket and admires the Venus de Milo. Most of the historical OUCH moments are pretty obvious, and I'll bet a truly knowledgeable person's head would explode from recognizing the details I missed.<br /><br />Okay, the BBC would never perpetrate this kind of fraud upon its public, but 'The Tudors' was made for Americans, and let's face it, we have never let facts mess up our own history.  Our own beloved President Reagan said 'facts are foolish things.\\"  We believe them impedimentary and unnecessary if they interfere with a good story--just take a look at the 5 Star reviews for this series.  Many completely dismiss the need to present history without distortion.  Accuracy is something that interferes with the plot and adds nothing. 0345457692|2.0|Having seen many good reviews of this book, I bought it for my wife, a mystery fan who's always on the lookout for new writers. She never got around to it, so I gave it a read. I'm very, very glad she did not waste her time on it.    I've read a lot of cyberpunk, and I know mysteries, both noir-ish and otherwise, pretty well, too. I'm open to new approaches that any genre might take -- in fact, I have a soft spot for mash-ups. However, Altered Carbon fails as a good noir thriller, a good cyberpunk novel, or a combination that somehow becomes greater than the sum of its separate genre components.    First, it's hard, as a long-time SF reader, to take seriously a book that starts with a troubled premise. While Morgan does depict some interesting and often credible repercussions of the \\"sleeving\\" technology that's the centerpiece of the book, he overlooks one major problem: Any instance of human consciousness is still a person. The book treats death, as long as you've been recorded and then recopied into another body, as meaningless. However, for the person (and a copy is a real person), death is as horrible as it always was.    Imagine you're sitting in a comfortable chair, and an exact copy of you walks into the room, gun drawn. The copy says, \\"I can give you 100% assurance that every aspect of your mind is perfectly re-created in me. In fact, I'll even remember everything that happens up to the point of me shooting you.\\" Your reaction won't be, \\"Fine, kill me.\\" The thinking, conscious, living creature you are will fight to defend itself, regardless of how good the copy is.    There are other problems with the thesis, such as the research in the last two decades that reveals how much our minds are tied into our bodies, in ways that makes it harder to imagine copying our minds as easily as making a ghost copy of a hard drive. But, science aside, Altered Carbon has lots of other problems.    Flat characters, including the main character. A parade of hot women who can'twait to sleep with the main character. The main character's solution of the mystery through leaps of logic. Boring scenes that add little to the story. (Drug trips, for example, are always boring in the re-telling.) Violence porn of an extreme variety. Expectations that we'll stick with the main character, even when he guns down people who, in his own telling, are just doing their jobs. Plot developments that, rather than being \\"seamless\\" (a favorite word of positive reviews here), are forced and clumsy. Read some of the other negative reviews for other serious problems.    In the end, the book gives itself away in the \\"About the author\\" blurb, which brags that the author has optioned the film rights for the book. Who cares? Why add that, other than to tell us how impressed we're supposed to be? Morgan clearly has had good marketing, and no small amount of luck, to get all the good press this book has received. There are far better books in both genres, and even SF mysteries (The Demolished Man, for starters), so I recommend giving them a try.
